                        rightTab-cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Room-temperature stationary sodium-ion batteries for large-scale electric energy storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Pan, Huilin; Hu, Yong-Sheng; Chen, Liquan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">6</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2338-2360</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1754-5706</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Room-temp. stationary sodium-ion batteries have attracted great attention particularly in large-scale elec. energy storage applications for renewable energy and smart grid because of the huge abundant sodium resources and low cost.  In this article, a variety of electrode materials including cathodes and anodes as well as electrolytes for room-temp. stationary sodium-ion batteries are briefly reviewed.  We compare the difference in storage behavior between Na and Li in their analogous electrodes and summarize the sodium storage mechanisms in the available electrode materials.  This review also includes some new results from our group and our thoughts on developing new materials.  Some perspectives and directions on designing better materials for practical applications are pointed out based on knowledge from the literature and our experience.  Through this extensive literature review, the search for suitable electrode and electrolyte materials for stationary sodium-ion batteries is still challenging.  However, after intensive research efforts, we believe that low-cost, long-life and room-temp. sodium-ion batteries would be promising for applications in large-scale energy storage system in the near future.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpqNKJTie6WmbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0li7_5HZ4u_xVg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhtFOisrzJ&amp;md5=2e02460553edc21e1ea8b73982ed63bb</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref4" class="rightTabRefNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit4" data-doi="10.1039/c3ee41031e"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Palomares, V.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Casas-Cabanas, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Castillo-Martinez, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Han, M. H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Rojo, T.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Update on Na-based Battery Materials.                 10.1039/c3ee41031e</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Update on Na-based battery materials. A growing research path</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Palomares, Veronica; Casas-Cabanas, Montse; Castillo-Martinez, Elizabeth; Han, Man H.; Rojo, Teofilo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">6</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2312-2337</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1754-5706</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  This work presents an up-to-date information on Na-based battery materials.  On the one hand, it explores the feasibility of two novel energy storage systems: Na-aq. batteries and Na-O2 technol.  On the other hand, it summarizes new advances on non-aq. Na-ion systems.  Although all of them can be placed under the umbrella of Na-based systems, aq. and oxygen-based batteries are arising technologies with increasing significance in energy storage research, while non-aq. sodium-ion technol. has become one of the most important research lines in this field.  These systems meet different requirements of energy storage: Na-aq. batteries will have a detg. role as a low cost and safer technol.; Na-O2 systems can be the key technol. to overcome the need for high energy d. storage devices; and non-aq. Na-ion batteries have application in the field of stationary energy storage.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqoMIYpy9lmPbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0li7_5HZ4u_xVg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhtFOisr%252FF&amp;md5=2cb5179714ac258186fecdd5b6c69452</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref5" class="rightTabRefNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit5" data-doi="10.1016/j.cclet.2017.11.039"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cui, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhan, T.-G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, K.-D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, D.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">The Recent Advances in Constructing Designed Electrode in Lithium Metal Batteries</span>. <i>Chin. Chem. Lett.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>28</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">2171</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">2179</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.cclet.2017.11.039</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">The recent advances in constructing designed electrode in lithium metal batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Cui, Jiecheng; Zhan, Tian-Guang; Zhang, Kang-Da; Chen, Dong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chinese Chemical Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">28</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2171-2179</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CCLEE7</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1001-8417</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review is given.  Li metal battery, one of the most promising candidates for high-energy-d. storage system, has attracted extensive interest due to its extremely high theor. specific capacity, low gravimetric d. and lowest neg. redox potential.  However, the formation of lithium dendrites, uncontrolled interfacial reactions, and huge vol. change during the cycles of charging and discharging lower the Coulombic efficiency and cause harsh safety issues; they are the major hurdles towards the practical applications of lithium metal batteries.  This paper presents the recent advances in Li metal battery and focuses on the functional hybrid electrodes, which are constructed to boost the electrochem. performance of metallic Li and suppress the growth of Li dendrites.  Their general design rules, fabrication strategies, enhanced performances, and potential applications are summarized and analyzed.  An outlook of the challenges and the potentials of Li metal battery is also provided, which facilitates the future development of Li metal batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrVQnmmC-sCJ7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0li7_5HZ4u_xVg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXhvFejs7nN&amp;md5=8adc7eb21b14e9d5117570a78fad14ff</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref6" class="rightTabRefNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit6" data-doi="10.1016/j.cclet.2017.09.065"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Q.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Two-Dimensional Vermiculite Separator for Lithium Sulfur Batteries</span>. <i>Chin.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Lithium-sulfur batteries have been considered as one of the most promising battery system for their high theor. energy d.  However, the lithium-sulfur batteries suffer from the dissoln. and diffusion of polysulfides, which induce parasitic reactions with lithium metal anodes.  The safety of lithium-sulfur batteries is also concerned with the risk of dendrite growth on lithium metal anodes.  To simultaneously address the challenges in the shuttle effect and safety problems, we demonstrate herein a two-dimensional vermiculite separator.  With the assembly of the 2D exfoliated vermiculite sheets, the vermiculite separator can suppress the diffusion of polysulfides across the separator through electrostatic interaction and steric hindrance.  Meanwhile, the inorg. sheets with high strength and Young's modulus prevent the penetration of lithium metal dendrite and potentially improve the safety of the system.  This work elucidates a promising strategy for safe and stable lithium sulfur batteries, and can also be extended to other electrochem. systems based on metal anodes.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq7Siy5VitpLrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0li7_5HZ4u_xVg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXhs1yjsL%252FN&amp;md5=2d28924bcca648bd3b7d2123982ff387</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref7" class="rightTabRefNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit7" data-doi="10.1002/advs.201600500"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiong, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hao, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Coordination of Surface-Induced Reaction and Intercalation: Toward a High-Performance Carbon Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Pure Sn and a homogeneous nanocomposite of Sn and graphite (C), denoted as Sn/C, have been studied as a suitable anode for Na ion batteries.         </div><div class="casAbstract">For a nonaq. sodium-ion battery (NIB), phosphorus materials have been studied as the highest-capacity neg. electrodes.  However, the large vol. change of phosphorus upon cycling at low voltage causes the formation of new active surfaces and potentially results in electrolyte decompn. at the active surface, which remains one of the major limiting factors for the long cycling life of batteries.  In this present study, powerful surface characterization techniques are combined for investigation on the electrode/electrolyte interface of the black phosphorus electrodes with polyacrylate binder to understand the formation of a solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) in alkyl carbonate ester and its evolution during cycling.  The hard XPS (HAXPES) anal. suggests that SEI (passive film) consists of mainly inorg. species, which originate from decompn. of electrolyte solvents and additives.  The thicker surface layer is formed during cycling in the additive-free electrolyte, compared to that in the electrolyte with fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) or vinylene carbonate (VC) additive.  The HAXPES and time-of-flight secondary ion mass spectroscopy (TOF-SIMS) studies further reveal accumulation of org. carbonate species near the surface and inorg. salt decompn. species.  These findings open paths for further improvement for the cyclability of phosphorus electrodes for high-energy NIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqcMIRYfr0XhbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lirAFG2sehc2A"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XisVygtbY%253D&amp;md5=fb5dc52d329e6ff1a088951643342c16</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref10" class="rightTabRefNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit10"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Metal sulfides are promising anode materials for sodium-ion batteries due to their large specific capacities.  The practical applications of metal sulfides in sodium-ion batteries, however, are still limited due to their large vol. expansion, poor cycling stability, and sluggish electrode kinetics.  In this work, a two-dimensional heterostructure of CoSx (CoS and Co9S8) quantum dots embedded N/S-doped carbon nanosheets (CoSx@NSC) is prepd. by a sol-gel method.  The CoSx quantum dots are in situ formed within ultrafine carbon nanosheets without further sulfidation, thus resulting in ultrafine CoSx particle size and embedded heterostructure.  Meanwhile, enriched N and S codoping in the carbon nanosheets greatly enhances the elec. cond. for the conductive matrix and creates more active sites for sodium storage.  As a result, the hybrid CoSx@NSC electrode shows excellent rate capability (600 mAh g-1 at 0.2 A g-1 and 500 mAh g-1 at 10 A g-1) and outstanding cycling stability (87% capacity retention after 200 cycles at 1 A g-1), making it promising as an anode material for high-performance sodium-ion batteries.  A CoSx@NSC//Na0.44MnO2 full cell is demonstrated, and it can deliver a specific capacity of 414 mAh g-1 (based on the mass of CoSx@NSC) at a c.d. of 0.2 A g-1.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq9OkEmDbmxnrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lirAFG2sehc2A"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXhvVaku7bJ&amp;md5=366aa605bfae1bf5095b737a7b5b3c40</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref13"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref13" class="rightTabRefNumLink">13</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit13" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2016.03.060"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, K.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), a potential alternative to lithium ion batteries (LIBs), have attracted remarkable attention recently due to the natural abundance and low-cost of sodium.  Here, we have presented a comprehensive study on combining electroless deposition with chem. dealloying to control the shell thickness and compn. of a red phosphorus (RP)@Ni-P core@shell nanostructure as a high performance anode for SIBs.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The capacity of hard carbon anodes in Na-ion batteries rarely reaches values beyond 300 mAh/g.  It is reported that doping POx into local structures of hard carbon increases its reversible capacity from 283-359 mAh/g.  It is confirmed that the doped POx is redox inactive by X-ray adsorption near edge structure measurements, thus not contributing to the higher capacity.  Two significant changes are obsd. of hard carbon's local structures caused by doping.  First, the (002) d-spacing inside the turbostratic nanodomains is increased, revealed by both lab. and synchrotron X-ray diffraction.  Second, doping turns turbostratic nanodomains more defective along ab planes, indicated by neutron total scattering and the assocd. pair distribution function studies.  The local structural changes of hard carbon are correlated to the higher capacity, where both the plateau and slope regions in the potential profiles are enhanced.  The study demonstrates that Na-ion storage in hard carbon heavily depends on carbon local structures, where such structures, despite being disordered, can be tuned toward unusually high capacities.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqdu855Pkblf7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lirAFG2sehc2A"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhtFOktLvL&amp;md5=1451c96d6ecdb7b2271ab49cd240507b</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref18"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref18" class="rightTabRefNumLink">18</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit18" data-doi="10.1002/anie.201403734"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jache, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Adelhelm, P.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Use of Graphite as a Highly Reversible Electrode with Superior Cycle Life for Sodium-Ion Batteries by Making Use of Co-Intercalation Phenomena</span>. <i>Angew.         </div><div class="casAbstract">This work reports that natural graphite is capable of Na insertion and extn. with a remarkable reversibility using ether-based electrolytes.  Natural graphite (the most well-known anode material for Li-ion batteries) has been barely studied as a suitable anode for Na rechargeable batteries due to the lack of Na intercalation capability.  Herein, graphite is not only capable of Na intercalation but also exhibits outstanding performance as an anode for Na-ion batteries.  The graphite anode delivers a reversible capacity of ≈150 mA-h/g with a cycle stability for 2500 cycles, and more than 75 mA-h/g at 10 A/g despite its micrometer-size (≈100 μm).  An Na storage mechanism in graphite, where Na+-solvent co-intercalation occurs combined with partial pseudocapacitive behaviors, is shown.  It is demonstrated that the electrolyte solvent species significantly affect the electrochem. properties, not only rate capability but also redox potential.  The feasibility of graphite in a Na full cell is also confirmed in conjunction with the Na1.5VPO4.8F0.7 cathode, delivering an energy of ≈120 W-h/kg while maintaining ≈70% of the initial capacity after 250 cycles.  This exceptional behavior of natural graphite promises new avenues for the development of cost-effective and reliable Na-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpH2c5ZOoUcVbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgRsWcc9ehLVg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXhvFGiurrN&amp;md5=0bb4c36145b59a3b9727155eca307655</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref20"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref20" class="rightTabRefNumLink">20</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit20" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201606023"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Noonan, O.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Tailored Yolk–Shell Sn@C Nanoboxes for High-Performance Lithium Storage</span>. <i>Adv.         </div><div class="casAbstract">We have systematically changed the no. of at. layers stacked in 2D SnO nanosheet anodes and studied their sodium ion battery (SIB) performance.  The results indicate that as the no. of at. SnO layers in a sheet decreases, both the capacity and cycling stability of the Na ion battery improve.  The thinnest SnO nanosheet anodes (two to six SnO monolayers) exhibited the best performance.  Specifically, an initial discharge and charge capacity of 1072 and 848 mAh g-1 were obsd., resp., at 0.1 A g-1.  In addn., an impressive reversible capacity of 665 mAh g-1 after 100 cycles at 0.1 A g-1 and 452 mAh g-1 after 1000 cycles at a high c.d. of 1.0 A g-1 was obsd., with excellent rate performance.  As the av. no. of at. layers in the anode sheets increased, the battery performance degraded significantly.  For example, for the anode sheets with 10-20 at. layers, only a reversible capacity of 389 mAh g-1 could be obtained after 100 cycles at 0.1 A g-1.  D. functional theory calcns. coupled with exptl. results were used to elucidate the sodiation mechanism of the SnO nanosheets.  This systematic study of monolayer-dependent phys. and electrochem. properties of 2D anodes shows a promising pathway to engineering and mitigating vol. changes in 2D anode materials for sodium ion batteries.  It also demonstrates that ultrathin SnO nanosheets are promising SIB anode materials with high specific capacity, stable cyclability, and excellent rate performance.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGok9B2_90thmbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgRsWcc9ehLVg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXhs1ags7s%253D&amp;md5=fe1ee09c0528efe249c2ef4f38511755</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref22"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref22" class="rightTabRefNumLink">22</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit22" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201603286"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, L.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Metallic tin has been considered as one of the most promising anode materials both for lithium (LIBs) and sodium ion battery (NIBs) because of a high theor. capacity and an appropriate low discharge potential.  However, Sn anodes suffer from a rapid capacity fading during cycling due to pulverization induced by severe vol. changes.  Here we innovatively synthesized pipe-wire TiO2-Sn@carbon nanofibers (TiO2-Sn@CNFs) via electrospinning and at. layer deposition to suppress pulverization-induced capacity decay.  In pipe-wire TiO2-Sn@CNFs paper, nano-Sn is uniformly dispersed in carbon nanofibers, which not only act as a buffer material to prevent pulverization, but also serve as a conductive matrix.  In addn., TiO2 pipe as the protection shell outside of Sn@carbon nanofibers can restrain the vol. variation to prevent Sn from aggregation and pulverization during cycling, thus increasing the Coulombic efficiency.  The pipe-wire TiO2-Sn@CNFs show excellent electrochem. performance as anodes for both LIBs and NIBs.  It exhibits a high and stable capacity of 643 mA h/g at 200 mA/g after 1100 cycles in LIBs and 413 mA h/g at 100 mA/g after 400 cycles in NIBs.  These results would shed light on the practical application of Sn-based materials as a high capacity electrode with good cycling stability for next-generation LIBs and NIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpWUZ-QZ7fVm7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgRsWcc9ehLVg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXntFejs7w%253D&amp;md5=fa57f9269273896b449ad581fcc03917</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref24"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref24" class="rightTabRefNumLink">24</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit24" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201505918"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bai, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qian, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Double-Walled Sb@TiO2–x Nanotubes as a Superior High-Rate and Ultralong-Lifespan Anode Material for Na-Ion and Li-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv. Sb@amorphous TiO2-x nanotubes are synthesized by the calcination of Sb2S3@TiO2 nanorods in Ar/H2 produced by the hydrolysis of tetra-Bu titanate (TBOT) on Sb2S3 nanorods.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Novel 3D ZnSb nanowire balls composed of ultra-thin nanowires were achieved through simple self-assembly method based on typical thermodn. in chem. vapor deposition (CVD) system. Then at. layer deposition is used to fabricate Ge@graphene@TiO2 core-shell nanofibers (Ge@G@TiO2 NFs) as anode materials for Li and Na ion batteries (LIBs and SIBs).         </div><div class="casAbstract">3D micro/nanobatteries in high energy and power densities are drawing more and more interest due to the urgent demand of them in integrating with numerous micro/nanoscale electronic devices, such as smart dust, miniaturized sensors, actuators, BioMEMS chips, and so on.  In this study, the electrochem. performances of 3D hexagonal match-like Si/Ge nanorod (NR) arrays buffered by TiN/Ti interlayer, which are fabricated on Si substrates by a cost-effective, wafer scale, and Si-compatible process are demonstrated and systematically investigated as the anode in sodium-ion batteries.  The optimized Si/TiN/Ti/Ge composite NR array anode displays superior areal/specific capacities and cycling stability by reason of their favorable 3D nanostructures and the effective conductive layers of TiN/Ti thin films.  Sodium-ion insertion behaviors are exptl. investigated in postmorphologies and elemental information of the cycled composite anode, and theor. studied by the first principles calcn. upon the adsorption and diffusion energies of sodium in Ge unit cell.  The preferential diffusion of sodium in Ge structure over in Si lattice is evidently proved.  The successful configuration of these distinctive wafer-scale Si-based Na-ion micro/nanobattery anodes can provide insight into exploring and designing new Si/Ge-based electrode materials, which can be integrated into micro-electronic devices as on chip power systems in the future.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpvy2j0ZbDFXrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljhd5uIF4d7Cw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXosFKktA%253D%253D&amp;md5=b50357ed97041d5386333e81d75c8574</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref31"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref31" class="rightTabRefNumLink">31</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit31" data-doi="10.1016/j.jallcom.2016.07.201"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jung, S.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The sodiation properties of alloy-type anode materials (Si, Ge, and Sn) were evaluated in terms of electrochem. energy storage using first-principles mol. dynamics calcns.  It is reported that Ge can deliver reasonably good performance in all aspects of sodium storage capability, mech. stability, and ion cond., when compared with Si and Sn.  The Ge anode has a strong thermodn. driving force for sodiation that is comparable to that of Sn and much stronger than that of Si, exhibits moderate vol. expansion and bulk modulus upon sodiation that are superior to those of Sn, and allows fast Na ion cond. that is comparable to that of Sn and is faster by three orders of magnitude than that of Si.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Red phosphorus (RP) is a promising anode material for lithium-ion batteries due to its earth abundance and a high theor. capacity of 2596 mA h g-1.  Although RP-based anodes for lithium-ion batteries have been reported, they were all in the form of carbon-P composites, including P-graphene, P-graphite, P-carbon nanotubes (CNTs), and P-carbon black, to improve P's extremely low cond. and large vol. change during cycling process.  Here, we report the large-scale synthesis of red phosphorus nanoparticles (RPNPs) with sizes ranging from 100 to 200 nm by reacting PI3 with ethylene glycol in the presence of cetyltrimethylammonium bromide (CTAB) in ambient environment.  Unlike the insulator behavior of com. RP (cond. of &lt;10 -12 S m-1), the cond. of RPNPs is between 2.62 × 10-3 and 1.81 × 10-2 S m-1, which is close to that of semiconductor germanium (1.02 × 10-2 S m-1), and 2 orders of magnitude higher than silicon (5.35 × 10-4 S m-1).  Around 3-5 wt % of iodine-doping was found in RPNPs, which was speculated as the key to significantly improve the cond. of RPNPs.  The significantly improved cond. of RPNPs and their uniform colloidal nanostructures enable them to be used solely as active materials for LIBs anodes.  The RPNPs electrodes exhibit a high specific capacity of 1700 mA h g-1 (0.2 C after 100 cycles, 1 C = 2000 mA g-1), long cycling life (∼900 mA h g-1 after 500 cycles at 1 C), and outstanding rate capability (175 mA h g-1 at the charge c.d. of 120 A g-1, 60 C).  Moreover, as a proof-of-concept example, pouch-type full cells using RPNPs anodes and Li(Ni0.5Co0.3Mn0.2)O2 (NCM-532) cathodes were assembled to show their practical uses.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqZ2BwgPjhTMrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljhd5uIF4d7Cw"> &gt;&gt;         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have been attracting intensive attention at present as the most promising alternative to lithium-ion batteries in large-scale elec. energy storage applications, due to the low-cost and natural abundance of sodium.  Elemental phosphorus (P) is a very promising anode material for SIBs, with the highest theor. capacity of 2596 mA h g-1.  Recently, there have been many efforts devoted to phosphorus anode materials for SIBs.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Graphene incorporation should be one effective strategy to develop advanced electrode materials for a sodium-ion battery (SIB).  Herein, the micro/nanostructural Sb/graphene composite (Sb-O-G) is successfully prepd. with the uniform Sb nanospheres (∼100 nm) bound on the graphene via oxygen bonds.  It is revealed that the in-situ-constructed oxygen bonds play a significant role on enhancing Na-storage properties, esp. the ultrafast charge/discharge capability.  The oxygen-bond-enhanced Sb-O-G composite can deliver a high capacity of 220 mAh/g at an ultrahigh c.d. of 12 A/g, which is obviously superior to the similar Sb/G composite (130 mAh/g at 10 A/g) just without Sb-O-C bonds.  It also exhibits the highest Na-storage capacity compared to Sb/G and pure Sb nanoparticles as well as the best cycling performance.  More importantly, this Sb-O-G anode achieves ultrafast (120 C) energy storage in SIB full cells, which have already been shown to power a 26-bulb array and calculator.  All of these superior performances originate from the structural stability of Sb-O-C bonds during Na uptake/release, which has been verified by ex situ X-ray photoelectron spectroscopies and IR spectroscopies.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqAWqDAYuZl6rVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh9ZH-yWP2tkA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XjvFylur8%253D&amp;md5=20ff4444ec296955ad0cbb06a02dfbef</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref37"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref37" class="rightTabRefNumLink">37</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit37" data-doi="10.1016/j.electacta.2016.06.031"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lim, C.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Amorphous Si (a-Si) powders were synthesized using various synthetic methods, and their electrochem. sodiation behaviors were studied.  These methods include (1) a chem. delithiation method where Si is 1st lithiated and then delithiated either in an aq. or org. soln., (2) a mech. method where cryst. Si is subjected to high-energy ball milling, and (3) a mech. fusion method where a Si-Sn composite with a Si-Sn at. ratio of 0.94:0.06 was synthesized through high-energy ball milling.  Com. Si monoxide (SiO) also was examd. for comparison.  Facile oxidn. of a-Si encountered in the methods (1) and (2) led to low sodiation capacity and huge 1st-cycle irreversible capacity, similar to those of SiO.  By contrast, the mech. fused Si-Sn composite, which contains a Sn shell that prevents the oxidn. of the partially amorphized Si core, exhibits a max. sodiation capacity of 161 mAh g-1 based on active material.  This is translated to an estd. sodiation capacity of ≤230 mAh g-1 for the a-Si component in the composite.  The presence of the Sn shell considerably reduces the 1st-cycle irreversible capacity and charge-transfer resistance, resulting in improved rate and cycle performance.  This suggests a new material design strategy that may enable the use of the a-Si for Na-ion battery anode applications.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrsrGCViVMVBLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh9ZH-yWP2tkA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhtVGiu7vM&amp;md5=62fdb9d37f75cf893d88153ec30d7648</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref38"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref38" class="rightTabRefNumLink">38</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit38" data-doi="10.1149/2.080403jes"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ellis, L.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The electrochem. alloying of sodium with silicon, lead and bismuth was studied by in-situ X-ray diffraction.  No evidence was found for sodium insertion into silicon at temps. up to 60°C.  Lead was found to catalytically decomp. electrolyte, hindering sodiation.  This could be avoided by applying a high current pulse to the cell to sodiate the lead surface.  Once the surface was sodiated, further sodiation of lead could proceed at low currents.                         rightTab-cas_cit39R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">39</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Charge carriers in rechargeable batteries: Na ions vs. Li ions</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hong, Sung You; Kim, Youngjin; Park, Yuwon; Choi, Aram; Choi, Nam-Soon; Lee, Kyu Tae</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  We discuss the similarities and dissimilarities of sodium- and lithium-ion batteries in terms of neg. and pos. electrodes.  Compared to the comprehensive body of work on lithium-ion batteries, research on sodium-ion batteries is still at the germination stage.  Since both sodium and lithium are alkali metals, they share similar chem. properties including ionicity, electronegativity and electrochem. reactivity.  They accordingly have comparable synthetic protocols and electrochem. performances, which indicates that sodium-ion batteries can be successfully developed based on previously applied approaches or methods in the lithium counterpart.  The electrode materials in Li-ion batteries provide the best library for research on Na-ion batteries because many Na-ion insertion hosts have their roots in Li-ion insertion hosts.  However, the larger size and different bonding characteristics of sodium ions influence the thermodn. and/or kinetic properties of sodium-ion batteries, which leads to unexpected behavior in electrochem. performance and reaction mechanism, compared to lithium-ion batteries.  This perspective provides a comparative overview of the major developments in the area of pos. and neg. electrode materials in both Li-ion and Na-ion batteries in the past decade.  Highlighted are concepts in solid state chem. and electrochem. that have provided new opportunities for tailored design that can be extended to many different electrode materials for sodium-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqHBS0wIkomorVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh9ZH-yWP2tkA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhtVWmtL%252FE&amp;md5=023136cc0e5ed94e18ce0d526a0000a4</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref40"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref40" class="rightTabRefNumLink">40</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit40" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2013.01.083"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Baggetto, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ganesh, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Meisner, R.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sn anodes show a rich structure and reaction chem. which the authors have studied.  Upon discharge five plateaus are obsd. corresponding to β-Sn, an unidentified phase (Na/Sn = 0.6), an amorphous phase (Na/Sn = 1.2), a hexagonal R-3m Na5Sn2, and fully sodiated I-43d Na15Sn4.  With charging there are six plateaus related to the formation of Na5Sn2 followed by the formation of amorphous phases and β-Sn.  Upon cycling the formation of metastable Na5Sn2 seems to be suppressed.  Theor. voltages calcd. from existing crystal structures using DFT provide a good match with const. current and quasi-equil. measurements (GITT).  Search for addnl. (meta)stable phases using cluster-expansion method predicts many phases lower in energy than the convex hull obtained from known structures, including the R-3m Na5Sn2 phase.  The presence of multiple phases in varying lattices with similar formation energy suggests why the reaction mechanism is nonreversible. 119Sn Mossbauer spectroscopy results indicate a decrease of the isomer shift with increasing Na/Sn content, which is less pronounced than for Li-Sn compds. likely due to the lower electropositivity of Na.  The electrode surface is terminated with an SEI layer rich in carbonates (Na2CO3 and Na CO3R) as evidenced by XPS.  After charge at 2 V, strong evidence for the formation of oxidized Sn4+ was obtained.  Subjecting the electrode to a rest after charge at 2 V reveals that aging in the electrolyte reduces the oxidized Sn4+ into Sn2+ and Sn0, and concomitantly suppresses the electrolyte decompn. represented by an anomalous discharge plateau at 1.2 V.  Thereby, the catalytic decompn. of the electrolyte during discharge is caused by nanosized Sn particles covered by oxidized Sn4+ and not by pure metallic Sn.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpyrhOO554AnLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh9ZH-yWP2tkA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXktFOktb0%253D&amp;md5=5bd2e34aea41e7840c8666db62bf7efe</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref41"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref41" class="rightTabRefNumLink">41</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit41" data-doi="10.1039/C5EE02524A"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gan, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ke, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhai, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Layered Phosphorus-Like GeP5: a Promising Anode Candidate with High Initial Coulombic Efficiency and Large Capacity for lithium Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Energy Environ.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">In this work, we for the first time investigate GeP5 as an anode material for lithium ion batteries (LIBs).  Using a facile high energy mech. ball milling (HEMM) method, we successfully synthesize pure GeP5 and GeP5/C nanocomposite at ambient temp. and pressure.  According to XRD Rietveld refinement and first principle calcns., GeP5 possesses a two-dimensional layered structure similar to that of black P and graphite, and a high cond. that is 10 000 and 10 times that of black P and graphite, resp.  Serving as novel anode materials, both GeP5 and its carbon composite deliver an unprecedented high reversible capacity of ca. 2300 mA h g-1, combined with a high initial coulombic efficiency of ca. 95%.  Ex situ XRD and CV tests demonstrate that GeP5 undergoes conversion and alloying type lithium storage mechanism and that its capacity is co-contributed to by both the Ge and P components.  In addn., GeP5/C exhibits superior cycle stability and excellent high-rate performance with a capacity of 2127 mA h g-1 at 5 A g-1.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium ion batteries (SIBs) are considered to be a promising alternative to lithium ion batteries because of the high abundance of sodium.  However, the scarcities of suitable anode materials severely hamper the development of SIBs.  Here, we synthesized a GeP5/C composite with binary sodium-reactive components on a large scale.  Theor., it can promise a capacity of 1888 mA h g-1 or 6891 mA h cm-3, which is the best record in anodes for SIBs reported so far.  In practice, the GeP5/C showed a low potential of ≈0.4 V vs.  Na+/Na with a smooth charge/discharge profile.  It delivered a large reversible capacity of 1250 mA h g-1 with a first coulombic efficiency of 93%.  Electrochem.-mechanism studies suggested that the formation of a GeP5 phase endowed a high first coulombic efficiency and synergetic effect between the sodiation of Ge and P.  This effect smoothly leveled the multistep plateaus and effectively reduced the polarization between charge/discharge.  When applied to a full cell by coupling a Na3V2(PO4)3/C cathode, the assembled Na3V2(PO4)3//GeP5 full cell showed a large capacity of 800 mA h g-1 with a high av. output voltage of 2.65 V.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Antimony trisulfide/reduced graphene oxide (Sb2S3-rGO) composite was successfully prepd. via a facile hot-injection route at a relatively low temp. using ethylene glycol (EG) as a nontoxic and cost-effective solvent.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries offer an attractive option for potential low cost and large scale energy storage due to the earth abundance of Na.  Red P is considered as a high capacity anode for Na-ion batteries with a theor. capacity of 2596 mA-h/g.  However, similar to Si in Li-ion batteries, several limitations, such as large vol. expansion upon sodiation/desodiation and low electronic conductance, have limited the performance of red P anodes.  To address the above challenges, the authors have developed a method to deposit red P nanodots densely and uniformly onto reduced graphene oxide sheets (P@RGO) to minimize the Na ion diffusion length and the sodiation/desodiation stresses, and the RGO network also serves as electron pathway and creates free space to accommodate the vol. variation of P particles.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Phosphorus is considered as a promising anode for Na-ion battery because of its high theor. capacity of 2595 mAh g-1.  In this study, two phosphorus-carbon (P-C) composites with well-controlled compns. and nanostructures of P and C have been developed: P@YP composite with P confined within porous structure of YP-80F carbon and P@CNT with unconfined P deposited on the surface of carbon nanotube.  The structure and electrochem. performance of these two composites have been studied to illustrate the effect of nanostructures of both C and P.  P@YP composite with appropriate amt. of P confined in nanopores can accommodate its large vol. change upon sodiation/desodiation and enable a stable solid-electrolyte interphase (SEI), ensuring an excellent long-term cycling stability with superior capacity retention of 92% after 100 cycles and 46% after 1000 cycles.  In contrast, the P@CNT composite with unconfined P nanostructures shows a rapid capacity decay with capacity retention of ∼ 40.6% after 100 cycles, most likely due to unstable SEI during cycling, caused by the large vol. changes of unconfined P in the P@CNT composite.  The well-designed nanostructured P-C composite with P confined within porous structure of carbon is demonstrated to greatly enhance the electrochem. performance, leading to promising long-term cycling stability.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrzjZhB2uHFi7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ligQLFEt4Se8g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXhsVOjt77N&amp;md5=3d3a2e634df272a1655713bea63694f8</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref49"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref49" class="rightTabRefNumLink">49</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit49" data-doi="10.1038/ncomms12122"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chao, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xia, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fan, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Savilov, S. V.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lin, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fan, H. J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shen, Z. X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Array of Nanosheets Render Ultrafast and High-Capacity Na-Ion Storage by Tunable Pseudocapacitance</span>. <i>Nat. Commun.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>7</i></span>, <span class="NLM_elocation-id">12122</span> <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1038/ncomms12122</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit49R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">49</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Array of nanosheets render ultrafast and high-capacity Na-ion storage by tunable pseudocapacitance</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Chao, Dongliang; Zhu, Changrong; Yang, Peihua; Xia, Xinhui; Liu, Jilei; Wang, Jin; Fan, Xiaofeng; Savilov, Serguei V.; Lin, Jianyi; Fan, Hong Jin; Shen, Ze Xiang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Communications</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">12122</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NCAOBW</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2041-1723</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries are a potentially low-cost and safe alternative to the prevailing lithium-ion battery technol.  However, it is a great challenge to achieve fast charging and high power d. for most sodium-ion electrodes because of the sluggish sodiation kinetics.  Here we demonstrate a high-capacity and high-rate sodium-ion anode based on ultrathin layered tin(II) sulfide nanostructures, in which a maximized extrinsic pseudocapacitance contribution is identified and verified by kinetics anal.  The graphene foam supported tin(II) sulfide nanoarray anode delivers a high reversible capacity of ∼1,100 mAh g-1 at 30 mA g-1 and ∼420 mAh g-1 at 30 A g-1, which even outperforms its lithium-ion storage performance.  The surface-dominated redox reaction rendered by our tailored ultrathin tin(II) sulfide nanostructures may also work in other layered materials for high-performance sodium-ion storage.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrVSw-KQ43s-LVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhDzELRHpJmgQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhtFSqtLbO&amp;md5=3f7ab452279e7159794a832183e58387</span></div></div></div></div></li></ol></li><li id="pane-m3" aria-labelledby="pane-m3" class="tab__pane tab__pane-suppInfo"><h2 class="supp-info-label">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article_supporting-info" id="rightTab-notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="rightTab-_i15">Supporting Information</h2></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.8b11103" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.8b11103</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Raman spectra of the GO, AB/p-rGO, and GeP<sub>5</sub>/AB/p-rGO composite; HRTEM image of the GeP<sub>5</sub>/AB/p-rGO composite; SEM image and elemental mapping of the GeP<sub>5</sub>/AB/p-rGO composite; comparison of CV curves, EIS spectra, and the Coulombic efficiency (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b11103/suppl_file/am8b11103_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b11103/suppl_file/am8b11103_si_001.pdf">am8b11103_si_001.pdf (1.37 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div><div id="rightTab-figshare-widget"></div><br><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="b84e6b6c-b4d0-4427-b286-3cf14d52d013" class="mb-4">

(function(){
	
	/*Event Tracking for RIS menu dropdown - Article pages*/

	$('.cit-download-dropdown_button').on('click',function(e)
	{	
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':'Main'});

	});


	$('.cit-download-dropdown_content ul li').on('click',function(e)
	{	
	//	e.preventDefault();
	    var idx = $(this).index();
	    
	    var label = '';
	    if (idx === 0 ) {
	    	label = 'Citation';
	    } else if (idx === 1 ) {
	    	label = 'Abstract';
	    } else if (idx === 2 ) {
	    	label = 'Reference';
	    } else if (idx === 3 ) {
	    	label = 'More Options';
	    }
	   
	   gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':label});
	});

	$('.header_contnav-next a').on('click', function(e){
        e.preventDefault();
        gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Right Arrow'});
	}); 

 	$('.header_contnav-pre a').on('click', function(e){
	    e.preventDefault();
	     gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Left Arrow'});
	}); 

	$('.share_button').on('click', function(e){
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Share'});
	});

	$("#pane-m1con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Figures'});
	});

	$("#pane-m2con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'References'});
	});

	$("#pane-m3con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Supporting Info'});
	});

	$('.tab__close').on('click', function(){
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Close'});
	}); 



})();

<script nonce="90da8fef6d36b325-SJC">
    document.addEventListener("DOMContentLoaded", function(){
    // Function to get a Human readable formatted date
    function getFormattedDateForMaintenance() {
        let today = new Date();
        let dd = String(today.getDate()).padStart(2, '0');
        //January is 0
        let mm = String(today.getMonth() + 1).padStart(2, '0'); 
        let yyyy = today.getFullYear();
        
        let daysOfWeek = ['Sunday', 'Monday', 'Tuesday', 'Wednesday', 'Thursday', 'Friday', 'Saturday'];
        let monthsOfYear = ['January', 'February', 'March', 'April', 'May', 'June', 'July', 'August', 'September', 'October', 'November', 'December'];

        let dayOfWeek = daysOfWeek[today.getDay()];
        let monthOfYear = monthsOfYear[today.getMonth()];

        return dayOfWeek + ', ' + monthOfYear + ' ' + dd + ', ' + yyyy;
    }
    
    // Insert the date into the .system-message-date element
    document.querySelector('.system-message-date').innerText = getFormattedDateForMaintenance();
});
</script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90da8fef6d36b325-SJC">

	/* 
	Create an in-page pop-up message, only for the InfoCentral homepage, that will gather email registrants for the library marketing list.
	 
	Needs the end point

var infoCentralCapture = (function() {

    //Detect mobile device
    window.mobilecheck = function() {
        var check = false;
        (function(a){if(/(android|bb\d+|meego).+mobile|avantgo|bada\/|blackberry|blazer|compal|elaine|fennec|hiptop|iemobile|ip(hone|od)|iris|kindle|lge |maemo|midp|mmp|mobile.+firefox|netfront|opera u|g560|gene|gf\-5|g\-mo|go(\.w|od)|gr(ad|un)|haie|hcit|hd\-(m|p|t)|hei\-|hi(pt|ta)|hp( i|ip)|hs\-c|ht(c(\-| |_|a|g|p|s|t)|tp)|hu(aw|tc)|i\-(20|go|ma)|i230|iac( |\-|\/)|ibro|idea|ig01|ikom|im1k|inno|ipaq|iris|ja(t|v)a|jbro|jemu|jigs|kddi|keji|kgt( |\/)|klon|kpt |kwc\-|kyo(c|k)|le(no|xi)|lg( g|\/(k|l|u)|50|54|\-[a-w])|libw|lynx|m1\-w|m3ga|m50\/|ma(te|ui|xo)|mc(01|21|ca)|m\-cr|me(rc|ri)|mi(o8|oa|ts)|mmef|mo(01|02|bi|de|do|t(\-| |o|v)|zz)|mt(50|p1|v )|mwbp|mywa|n10[0-2]|n20[2-3]|n30(0|2)|n50(0|2|5)|n7(0(0|1)|10)|ne((c|m)\-|on|tf|wf|wg|wt)|nok(6|i)|nzph|o2im|op(ti|wv)|oran|owg1|p800|pan(a|d|t)|pdxg|pg(13|\-([1-8]|c))|phil|pire|pl(ay|uc)|pn\-2|po(ck|rt|se)|prox|psio|pt\-g|qa\-a|qc(07|12|21|32|60|\-[2-7]|i\-)|qtek|r380|r600|raks|rim9|ro(ve|zo)|s55\/|sa(ge|ma|mm|ms|ny|va)|sc(01|h\-|oo|p\-)|sdk\/|se(c(\-|0|1)|47|mc|nd|ri)|sgh\-|shar|sie(\-|m)|sk\-0|sl(45|id)|sm(al|ar|b3|it|t5)|so(ft|ny)|sp(01|h\-|v\-|v )|sy(01|mb)|t2(18|50)|t6(00|10|18)|ta(gt|lk)|tcl\-|tdg\-|tel(i|m)|tim\-|t\-mo|to(pl|sh)|ts(70|m\-|m3|m5)|tx\-9|up(\.b|g1|si)|utst|v400|v750|veri|vi(rg|te)|vk(40|5[0-3]|\-v)|vm40|voda|vulc|vx(52|53|60|61|70|80|81|83|85|98)|w3c(\-| )|webc|whit|wi(g |nc|nw)|wmlb|wonu|x700|yas\-|your|zeto|zte\-/i.test(a.substr(0,4))) check = true;})(navigator.userAgent||navigator.vendor||window.opera);
              return check;
        };

    var _isMobile = window.mobilecheck();
    if (_isMobile) {
        $('#gdpr-message-modal').addClass('mobile');
    }

    var emailValid = "",
        time = 3000,
        btnStatus = true,
        errorStatus = false,
        errormessage = "<p class='gdpr_err'><b>Please Enter a valid email address<b><p>";

    function init() {
        setTimeout(function() {
            var cookieExist = getCookie("gdpr");
            if (cookieExist) {
                //console.log("gdpr cookie exist");
                return false;
            } else {
                //console.log("gdpr cookie does not exist")
                gdprMessage();
            }
            //Events
            $('#closebtn').on("click", hideForm);

            //Handle submit
            $('#gdpr-message-btn').on("click", function(event) {
                handleClick();
            });

            // //Hide if clicked outside div - TO DO stop handling click after submit
            // $(document).click(function(event) {
            // 	event.stopPropagation();
            //   console.log(event.target);
            //   if (!$(event.target).closest("#gdpr-message-content").length) {
            //     $("body").find("#gdpr-message-modal").hide();
            //     $(this).off();
            //   }
            // });
            $('#gdpr-con-btn').click(function() {
                setCookie("gdpr", "true");
                hideForm();
            });
        }, time);
    }

    //Show the message
    function gdprMessage() {
        //console.log("Show GDPR Message");
        var tMessage, emailInput, messageButton, ewrap;

        $('#gdpr-message-modal').show();
    }

    function handleClick() {
        //console.log($("#gdpr-input").val());
        emailValid = validateEmail($("#gdpr-input").val());
        if (emailValid) {
            //Submit the form
            //Hide form
            if ($('.gdpr_err')) {
                $('.gdpr_err').hide();
            }

            $("#gdpr-message-content")
                //If successful then...
            setCookie("gdpr", "true");
            //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid + " submiting form");
        } else {
            //Show error message
            gdprErrorMessage();
            //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid);
        }
    }

    function gdprErrorMessage() {
        if (errorStatus) {
            return false;
        } else {
            errorStatus = true;
            $("#gdpr-message-content").append(errormessage);
        }
    }

    function validateEmail(email) {
        var re = /^(([^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+(\.[^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+)*)|(".+"))@((\[[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\])|(([a-zA-Z\-0-9]+\.)+[a-zA-Z]{2,}))$/;
        return re.test(String(email).toLowerCase());
    }

    function setCookie(name, value) {
        var expires = "";
        expires = "; expires=Fri, 31 Dec 9999 23:59:59 GMT";
        document.cookie = name + "=" + (value || "") + expires + "; path=/";
    }

    function getCookie(name) {
        var match = document.cookie.match(new RegExp(name + '=([^;]+)'));
        if (match) return match[1];
    }

    function hideForm() { //And set cookie
        $('#gdpr-message-modal').hide();
        setCookie("gdpr", "false");
        gtag('event', 'GDPR Modal', { 'event_category': 'Pubs GDPR', 'event_label':'GDPR Dismissal'});
    }

    return {
        init: init
    }
})();

$(document).ready(infoCentralCapture.init);
</script>


                    
                        



        
        <script nonce="90da8fef6d36b325-SJC">
var currentJournal = "ACS Applied Materials &amp;amp; Interfaces";
$(".citationLinks > a").click(function(e){
    if($(this).text() === "ACS Full Text ") {
        gtag('event', 'ACS Full Text', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Crossref") {
        gtag('event', 'Crossref', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "CAS") {
        gtag('event', 'CAS', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "PubMed") {
        gtag('event', 'PubMed', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Google Scholar") {
        gtag('event', 'Google Scholar', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).hasClass("issue-item_open-url") === true) {
        gtag('event', 'OpenURL', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
});
</script>

                    
                        



        
        <script nonce="90da8fef6d36b325-SJC">
$(document).ready(function(){
    var cookie = "";
    var uri = "/doi/full/10.1021/html_original_uri?id=123";
    console.log("ACSPubs2 start");
    $.getJSON('https://extreme-ip-lookup.com/json/', function(data, status) {
            if ( status == 'success' ) {
                $.each(data, function(i, field){
                    cookie=cookie.concat(i+' = '+field+" - ");
                });
                
            } else {
                    cookie=status;
            }

            cookie1 = cookie;
            cookie = cookie1 + "; path=/; domain=.acs.org";
            //document.cookie = "ACSPubs2= "+ btoa(cookie) ;
            $.cookie("ACSPubs2", btoa(cookie), {path: '/'}); // Session cookie valid across the entire site.
            //TODO send cookie val to the controller for logging
            console.log("ACSPubs2 success");
    })
    .fail(function(xx, status, err){
        cookie="error: 127.0.0.1";
        //document.cookie = "ACSPubs2= "+ btoa(cookie) ;
        $.cookie("ACSPubs2", btoa(cookie), {path: '/'}); // Session cookie valid across the entire site.
        console.log("ACSPubs2 failed");
        //TODO send cookie val to the controller for logging
    });
});
